=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A really nice, beautiful and tactile game with simple mechanisms and stacks of options.  If you're a Feld fan like we are, you're probably going to like this one too.

The game revolves around a deck of 165 unique cards, 33 in each of five colours, many of which won't be used in any particular game.

Each round consists of:

•	All players draw cards from either of two piles to bring their hand to five.  You can see what colour card you're choosing, but you don't get to look at the faces until you've drawn all of them.  (The decks are held in neat slanted boxes that allow players to draw cards without accidentally revealing cards further down the deck)
•	The five dice are rolled, and Macao-style, affect all players and are used by all players.
•	In turn, each player plays a card until everyone has just one remaining (which will form the start of the next hand).

Because there are six things you could do with a given card, each action you choose means you miss out on five you could have chosen.  But despite the large number of combinations of options, we felt our goals remained pretty clear to us so the game didn't suffer from AP.

Players use their cards to do one of:

•	Build a building - by paying a worker of the matching colour, and placing the card face down into their play area
•	Recruit a person - by paying the cost in guilders for that person and placing the card face up on a building card
•	Build a section of canal - by playing a card of the required colour and paying guilders for the section built
•	Obtain two workers - by discarding a card of a matching colour to the workers
•	Obtain some guilders - by discarding the card and taking a number of guilders equal to the die roll of that colour
•	Removing one threat marker - by discarding a card of a matching colour to the threat (threat markers are given to all players as a result of the dice rolls.  They're a mechanism to potentially disrupt plans and give the players something more to worry about)

Lots of ways to earn victory points, and many possible routes.

If it helps you decide whether you'd like Bruges, of the Feld games we have played, this is currently our order of preference:

Castles of Burgundy, Notre Dame, Bruges, Trajan, Macao, Rialto.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Prototypical Feld, on the lighter end of his scale, this is a "point-salad" game with an element of punitive measures thrown in.  Theme?  Forget it, non-existent, but it scales very well from 2-4 players, it has nice artwork, and it's easy breezy to learn and play a game (or two).

The goal of the game is to score the most points before game end, which is somewhat variable (game enters the end phase when one of the draw decks runs out).  At the start of each round, the dice are rolled and allocated (a new hallmark of Feld games it to make innovative use of dice, and it occurs once again although to a lesser and minor extent in this game)- the dice, which are coloured and correspond to the colours of character cards, dictate the values of money for the round, and the type of penalty, if any for that round.  Then you can choose, in turn order, to advance on the prestige track (this is one of several ways to score points), keeping in mind that this costs money and money is very tight but it also can potentially give you an opportunity for a "leading category" point score (another way to score points).  

You then draw a set hand of cards of which you will play all but one, and you see the colour (this corresponds to dice actions/costs), but not the effect- you also can draw from 2 different piles and have some control over the speed/length of the game.  On your turn, you play a card to activate an action, which of course is generally colour limited: a) get money (refer to the die pips of that colour), b) build a house, which requires a worker of that colour (another way to score points and is required to house workers, think "In the Year of the Dragon"), c) play a personality card, which requires money, is another way to score points and gives benefits some immediate, some reusable (this is the part that can be frustrating if you can't get any card synergy), d) fight an event (scores small points and avoids pain), e) collect workers (colour determined), f) build canals, which is colour limited, requires money, and scores points.

At the end of the round, you survey the lay of the land and can score points for having a majority in: a) prestige track, b) workers housed, c) canals built.  These points are significant but can only be scored once each.

This is a solid game effort, but it can be a bit off-putting as many of the card actions are "take-that" in nature, the card draws can be frustrating, and the events can be punitive and while you can work to mitigate them, you can really only do this if you can get those card colours, which *shouldn't* be hard, but sometimes can be.  It does scale well, plays quick, and is easy to learn.  I wouldn't be surprised (or disappointed really) if this wins the fluff SdJ award.

Own the [boardgame=154678]City on the Zwin[/boardgame] expansion and the [boardgame=166504]Pets[/boardgame] promo.

RATING: 7
I was a bit disappointed with my first play of this game.  I'm a big fan of Stefan Feld and usually find his games interesting mechanically, but this one didn't feel like it brought anything new to the table.  It feels similar to any number of other tableau building card games out there, like Race for the Galaxy, San Juan, Innovation or Seasons.  The use of the board is minimal and doesn't really add much to the game.  It didn't help that in our game I got a strong card early on that pretty much dictated what strategy I would follow and led to an easy win by a wide margin.

I'd definitely like to play it again to see if my opinion changes, but for now, this definitely seems the weakest of Feld's 2013 releases.

UPDATE 8/12/14: My second play was more fun and had a much closer result.  I still feel like there's not a lot innovative about the game, but it has good fun factor and is on the shorter end of the Feld scale in terms of playing time.

RATING: 9.06
Abstract:
A multi-used card board game by Stefan Feld. There are cards and dice for each of the 5 colors. At the start of each round, all dice are rolled and each player draws up to 5 cards. Threat tokens are distributed depending on the rolled dice. Each player may pay money to advance on the reputation track. Turn-wise, each player plays a card (up to 4).

Possible card actions:
a) get money (depending on pips of the same colored die)
b) get henchmen (depending on pips of the same colored die)
c) discard one threat token (of the same color)
d) build a house (and discard a henchman of the same color)
e) build a canal (discard a card of the same color and pay money)
f) place a card (in an empty house and pay money)

Pros:
+ Multi-used cards combined with dice(!)
+ Mitigation of randomness is a big part of the game
+ Choosing colored cards from two separate decks sounds insignificant, but it actually is important to keep one's options open
+ Indirect player interaction by racing for majority markers is brilliant
+ Big amount of cards and all of them are unique (however, most differ slightly)
+ Engine building is fun and different each game
+ Good mix between tactical game (decision-making on a round-basis) and strategical game (engine-building, money management, henchmen management, threat management, and keeping one card for the next round)
+ Threat management (push your luck)
+ Theme is quite well integrated (compared to other games by Stefan Feld)
+ Plays well with all player counts (more competition but also down-time on higher player counts)
+ Nearly family-friendly (it is a gateway plus game)
+ Quick set-up
+ Quick game
+ Easy to teach

Neutrals:
# Artwork is okay
# Scoring happens during the round, between rounds, and at the end of the game

Cons:
- Lucky (last round) draws are possible (but evens out over the course of the game)
- Most of the player interaction is of take-that variety (but the number of take-that cards is rather low)

Thoughts:
I love the combination of multi-used cards and dice in Bruges. The game offers a lot of new and interesting decisions each round. Randomness, and how to mitigate it, is a big part of the game. Compared to other Stefan Feld games, Bruges is a rather quick and "light" game, but still deep. 
Note: Bruges is one of few games by Stefan Feld that got a proper expansion.

Similar games:
* Glory to Rome, Oh My Goods, Race for the Galaxy, San Juan (because of multi-used cards)

Player: 2-4 (5 with expansion) (best: 3-4) | Time: 60m

RATING: 6
I accidentally played a Feld.

Bijan's arsonist (aka take a red token) strategy failed to stop me winning with some combination of soulless, ugly cards. 

The company was good and the actual game elements here have great potential; mainly because the cards enjoy a myriad of possibilities and you therefore have a lot of control... but the theme and execution of Bruges is so incredibly dull even the surprisingly prevalent dickery just comes down to adding three blue things together and losing one of your tokens or points.

And don't say "ooh, but canals are totally Bruges" - you could set this game in Birmingham and have the little "reputation" meeples walk up New Street towards the bullring or something. If you don't think those canals count, you could just replace them with endless, pointless roadworks and you're finished.
You'd probably have to update the card art for some of the tedious old timey individuals on them so they instead show some tedious modern people, but the game would be identical. 

Yeah, there you go, publisher of this game - there's a challenge for you: create a game unique to a British town or city that couldn't just be moved to a Belgian city with a slight facelift. Maybe base it on Slough? 

Yeah, Slough: The Boardgame. 
Use multi purpose cards with pictures of roadsigns on in order to spend pointless "effort" cubes to drag yourself to an industrial estate and then move around an endless ringroad and occasionally attempt suicide. That's Slough, The Boardgame. 
It could have a "Crushing Ennui" track. 
You could put David Brent on the box, because that's all anyone bloody knows about Slough, isn't it? Slough.

In the future dystopian society we face following the inevitable rise of Pan-European statehood*, a terrifying nanny state will force all games to be produced in packages as terrifyingly and soul crushingly bland as Bruges, lest imagination and excitement at a theme lead to a popular uprising against their authoritarian masters.

TLDR: I liked it more than I expected, to be honest.

* #irony

RATING: 5.9
The Master has done it again! No, no, not Stefan Feld; Michael Menzel. This is probably the most beautiful example of his artwork yet. Somehow, though, the art, which portrays Bruges in the 15th century, ended up in a game which has nothing to do with Bruges in the 15th century. Hmmm: even the game is called "Bruges". Strange. Must have been some mix up at the packing plant.

Speaking of art, the only negative is that the purple and brown colours are very hard to distinguish. I think it would be impossible for a colour-blind person to play this well.

Mechanically speaking, Bruges is near flawless. There is just enough luck in it to keep it from being dull. Additionally, in most games like this, the person who has memorized the cards has a decided advantage. But memorizing the cards is kinda pointless, since not all the cards will be used in a game. That ensures a high level of re-playability. As well, you can see the end of the game approaching as the card decks shrink, so that keeps a good level of tension.

However, it is multi-player solitaire; and (possibly due to the joke of a theme; and we literally were making jokes about it as we played!) it just doesn't grab me. Mind you, the only Stefan Feld game that did grab me was Macao, so take my rating with a grain of salt!

RATING: 5.5
Only a single play, but I'm enjoying the wealth of options available. 

The game plays itself a bit, what with the randomness of the cards (common complaint), but it doesn't overstay its welcome and has a lot of replayability. 

Per the "plays itself a bit" comment, what I mean is you inflexibly have to play 4 cards in a round, so if you're down to two cards, you're prevented from holding back if you like both options. This, to me, feels like the game is forcing you into an action, which is why I use the expression above. Thankfully, there are enough things one wants to do (mitigate threat, build canals, generate money, etc) that it hasn't been much of a problem. 

Expansion owned but still unplayed. 

-- 12/30/14 (7.5 then)

7/11/17 Update (5.5 downgrade):

Three years later, I have many plays of this under my belt. For whatever reason I don't wish to edit my original commentary, there is something refreshingly pure about that naive perspective, but suffice to say my opinion of both the core game and expansion has come down a notch. Largely this is due to the wildly random nature of the card draw. If I can't be too put out due to a loss ("oh well, I never got that blue I needed") then I can't get too excited about a win either. Milk meet toast.  

The expansion is module based, but only the boats and cards are a positive addition.

RATING: 6
The engine of Bruges is a big deck of character cards and the multiple things you can do with those cards.  The dice in the game serve to modify judgements about what's best to do with each colour of card each round. As ever with Feld, there are multiple ways to score.  

The luck of the card draw and of those dice seems significant.  Sometimes card combos will fall into place and at others they won't.  Sure you can change your plan, make the best possible use of the five cards in your hand, but still you're trailing the player with better luck.  

For the most part you are building your own tableau and digging your own canals.  There are, however, a few cards that impact on opponents.  The three bonus tiles are an odd feature.  Their bonuses often seem to be collected early and then tied positions or an inability to progress result in little or no change.  

Games of Bruges can come to an abrupt end before there has been much development.  By the same token, the game is light and quick and I found some degree of fun in it.

Production quality is to Hans im Gluck and Z-Man's best standards.  I found the artwork quite pleasing and evocative.

RATING: 6.5
Yet another Stefan Feld game and I think that with this one I completed my campaign of playing every Feld game made after 2010. This one feels less re-engineered than Amerigo, it has the fun factor and plays nicely overall, with players having enough choices for the game to be interesting but not too many so that casual gamers can enjoy.

The characters represent the interesting part of the game and while they bring interesting abilities, they are not balanced. I won my first game by chance, drawing a character which score alone 16 VP (4 from on its own and 12 from the ability). This is something unexpected and a quite disappointing. Also, there are several characters with specific abilities which give a huge advantage in the beginning of the game.

I believe that between player who know the game the difference is made by the lucky draw of characters rather than superior strategy.

RATING: 6
Pleasant enough card effects game that plays quickly and breezily.  Nothing too compelling here in terms of game play, but still, in its own way, this game is an interesting illustration of how to design a decent card effects game.  I am just not a card effects game lover;  as such, given all the other games I might play, not sure I would choose to play this "decent" game again.

Initial rating is 7.  Only played about 3/4 of a game (missed the first 1/4).

Upgraded from 7 to an 8 after first full play.

RATING: 9.4
Elegant, beautiful game, one of Feld's very best, I'd place it somewhere between Trajan and Castles of Burgundy. I love how the cards can be used for normal actions, or Houses, or Persons to house in those houses, which will give you variable abilities throughout the game. The board is colorful, mechanics intuitive and simple, and while it may be considered a 'lighter Feld', that is in no way a negative connotation for me, since it's a game that can be enjoyed again and again, and still gives me a similar feel to some of his heavier designs. The dice and threat tokens lend it a little bit of a Troyes feeling.

RATING: 7.8
Feld's successful take on the "Race for the Galaxy" style Tableau builder.  There is a stronger focus on economics than other games of the same type and like any card game you can be undone by card selections with no synergy. 

The game's theme wont inspire most people, but there art is quite nice.  There are a TON of cards in this game and each one has a unique portrait on it which is impressive. 

Overall I found it to be really satisfying.  There is a lot of variety in the gameplay and plenty of interesting decisions to mull over.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The third Feld opus of 2013 I tried, after the great Bora Bora and the light Rialto, is another excellent game, again in the lighter side of the canon. I guess this can easily be compared to Notre Dame.

The game plays really fast (you can play a 2-player game in half a hour), and has a fair share of luck in card drawing (particularly if you draw, or don't draw, the right string of colors), but this has not been a problem so far: the 165 unique cards make the game's replayability very high, and the short duration makes the luck tolerable.

The game is very feldian, as one might expect: different ways to score points, clever and different use of dice, lots of interesting decisions. Everything packed in a very simple package, that could appeal even to non hardcore gamers.

German copy sold after the release of the italian version.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.95
Bruges is a great game.  It is a multi-use card game in which cards can be used in 6 different ways althought only for one use does the card have any significance other than its colour.  Like Race for the Galaxy there is a lot of randomness in the game but the winning player will probably be the player who best utilised their opportunities whilst mitigating their risks the best.  Its really easy to teach and learn and is a lot of fun.  Need more games to compare it with other Felds.  It shares a lot with Macao in that each utilise cards and dice in similar and interesting ways although Macao is more about the dice and this is more about the cards.  For a lot of gamers this will be way too random but with its quick playing time, large number of interesting decisions and ways of countering the randomness make it shine for me.

RATING: 2
The dry Bruges is a textbook example of the eurogames’ golden rule: when playing, you’re allowed to take some actions but never the ones you’d really need, insomuch that you quickly feel like you’re panting a few moves behind. 

Everything in the game is made to glorify rules, mechanisms and optimization above everything else, leaving no room for the the warm, human rewards of a little theme, jokes or even chit-chatting. 

It’s like the authors were saying: You’re playing my game and that’s a deadly serious matter, son! How would you belittle it by adding a little light-heartedness?

It's tough to weight the gameplay, since it's mediumish, but figuring out what to do is definitely not. I really enjoyed the game. It's a euro game with a lot of screw-em-over tactics thrown in, which is refreshing. One player can play a card or do something that causes the rest of the players to gain some sort of problem. RATING: N/A
Bruges has many of Feld's hallmark mechanisms - multi-use cards, lots of ways to score points, valuation that changes over time, and random punishments that must be avoided.  The cards are at the heart of Bruges, and they can be used in 5 (or 6?) different ways. When drawing cards, you have 2 piles to pick from, and the card backs show the card color, which gives a small measure of control, since the color matters for about 3 of the ways you might play a card.  

The game quickly devolves into an exercise of triage - look through your hand and decide which cards you must play this round for certain things, then decide how you will play the other cards to support this.  For example, you might decide that 2 of your cards have people on them (which grant special abilities) that are crucial to your strategy.  You will then want to allocate 2 cards you don't need toward building houses for those people, so you need to figure out which 2 cards are your least needed.  Repeat as necessary.

Our first play of Bruges was rather lackluster.  My wife ran away with it after getting a particularly nice combo of people cards.  I think that more experience might mitigate this, but it still seems likely to happen regularly.  Ultimately, it did not grab us and we failed to return to it because there were always options we wanted to play more.  Eventually I traded it away.

I think those who like optimization puzzles will like Bruges, particularly if you like building combos.

:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ Great, light-medium weight multi-uses card tactical point-salad game with smooth gameplay.
++ Great card graphic design, that is clean and clear.
++ The game have "Threat Tokens" that are randomly generated by the dice of each turn that affect all players' short-term strategy (These are actually just a third of the threat, the threat being effective only when you have all 3 third threat tokens, and thus we can see these threats coming and can either counter them or mitigate their effect).
++ Good replayability from the fact that each game use just a part of all the cards, the variable card draw order and also because of the multi-uses cards.
++ There is one expansion available for this game (which is useful to refresh the game when we have overplayed it... but unfortunately, it's so hard to find!).
++ Great aesthetics!


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- Really good 2-players game, but probably better at 3-4 players, as some people cards have better effect at higher player number.
-- The game is more luck-based than I would like, thus you have to have a more tactical mindset when approaching this game (as you will not do what you want to do, but what you can do with the card you get).

RATING: 8.6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The usual Feld points salad format, but this time, with a whole bunch of characters that give special abilities mixed in!

While I'm not generally a huge fan of tons of special abilities, the ones used here are easy to manage and while there's some combos that work well together, it doesn't become a ridiculous exercise in tracking character abilities since you'll likely only have a few at your disposal at any given time.

As is fairly typical for Feld, he also finds a neat way to integrate dice that doesn't feel super dicey.  The dice dictate the flow of bad events that will happen to players, as well as dictate how easy or difficult it will be to get money.

In the end, some Feld fans may feel there is too much randomness in the card draw and the dice rolls, taking some control out of players hands.  However, I really enjoyed the unpredictability these elements had, rolling with the punches as I tried to execute a strategy.

*Note: This game is only a 10 when played with the expansion, which fixes canal building into something that is actually worthwhile.  Canal building is nearly always a losing strategy without it.  I'd still rate the game an 8 without the expansion, but it really completes the game into what it should have been to begin with.

RATING: 6
I’m not a huge Feld fan, but I like CoB and Trajan quite a bit as well as Bora Bora. However, I think Feld makes a lot of average games: Speicherstadt, Macao, Notre Dame etc. all fell flat, and Brügge does too. It’s not a bad game; it’s just not particularly good either… It lacks interesting decisions and tension.

Brügge is not the typical point salad game which Feld has become famous (or feared…) for, but the point scoring still feels very uninspiring. You get some points for moving up on the track, for building canals and for the persons you hire. Meh. These persons vary a lot in strength and usability, but my biggest gripe is probably that you cannot really make a definitive strategy as you don’t know which cards you’ll be getting later in the game, so it’s a little hard to make those good card combos which will win you the game! You only keep a single card from round to round, so planning ahead is difficult. The best mechanism in Brügge is the disasters; they add some tension and relatively important decision making to the game, and there are some mean cards which the other players can play to make you get the last piece for the disaster to happen (you need 3 before the disaster strikes) – this can be very crippling, but I guess you just have to take it into account, so it’s definitely not a negative point.

The artwork is nice (though perhaps slightly too brown for my taste), so why did they choose to make the game board so small? And now that they chose this, then why did they choose to make the box so relatively big? Oh well, that’s not a complaint about the game, it just bugs me; it could easily fit in a Speicherstadt sized box or even a smaller one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
Bruges is one of my favorite games, not only it's a Feld's game, the replendent cards and game boards design, improvised strategy planning, tense dice scenarios, etc. Though Bruges shares the same problem of all card-driven games: random card drawing, but the randomness does not affect the game significantly, instead Bruges is well-balanced between strategy depth and randomness.

Many people - include some of my friends - thinks Bruges is a bad-designed strategy game: players can't really make any long-term strategy due to the randomness of card drawing, which rise the pain index of strategy planning. In my opinion Bruges is a typical Feld's game which does not encourage players to focus on single strategy developement. Player need to realize Bruges is a resource-exchanging game which require players to consider: based on current dice rolls, how to optimize the cards I just draw to gain more points, which action to choose for each card to perform best usage, that strategy struggle is the essence of Bruges.  The player's plan should mainly focus on "current turn", adaptive players are always rewarded greatly while stubborn players find themselves stuck in the dead end. But I also think Bruges is a game with long-term strategies, reflect on "which card to keep to the next turn" and "which color of cards to choose": why choose these cards? why choose these colors? what's their future benefit. Player still need to come out a long-term plan to achieve victory.

The cost efficiency of the 2nd canal is broken in the base game, however the problem is improved in the expansion, the motivation lack of building canal is well compensated by the ships.(although i think some of the abilities are broken as well, i.e. extra action and the prestige track+1)

Overall, Bruges is a very enjoyable strategy game, your still suffer from bad draws or poor dice rolls, but they are not the end of the world. An improvised player always get great return. Bruges has all the key elements of Feld's game: various strategy paths, appropriate randomness, with very weak thematic (I still can't convince myself that every character is related to the ability instead of randomly matched). If you are a Stefan Feld's fan, can tolerate moderate strategy randomness, Bruges will be a great choice. If you like long-term planning, like to accomplish your plan step by step, you can always play Trajan or Puerto Rico.

Pros:
+Encourage improvisation in strategies.
+Beautiful art design.
+Cards/dices/colors-driven mechanics are amazing.
Cons: 
- Lack of theme.
- Moderate Randomness.

(Highly recommend playing with the expansion)

The expansions adds some new modules and cards. I definitely like all of the new cards and the market module. I feel the ships take some of the tightness away from the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
A simple, rythmical game by Stefan Feld, almost soothingly so; and one of his first titles which felt like a coherent whole in which no particular mechanism takes the central limelight.

Game-technically the game revolves around incomplete information card draw decisions for a subsequent turn, and then making the best with the small hand you've been dealt. Probably a good player is able to circumvent the luck of the draw a little and be able to use his hand no matter what turns up (not unlike Kingdom Builder) but at the same there's quite a bit of variety in the cards which suggests that the analogy can only be taken so far. Whatever the case: Brugge is friendly to play, but is not entirely simple either, which probably accounts for a good deal of its popularity. 

Does the game attempt to create novel interaction? Of course not. There is hardly any, when it comes to that. That is sad, but neither should I completely dismiss the effort put into this game: it's not easy to create a game of this complexity level. I can see myself joining in a few more times in the future.

RATING: 8
I love this game, but it's not without problems. Bruges is a little too tactical, less strategic than I would like, but it's a joy to play and easy to teach. Every once in a while though, there are times when you really need a purple card in order to get purple workers to run your engine or build that crucial canal, and you just can't draw a purple card. That can be extremely frustrating. The game would be less frustrating, I think, if there was a way to transform any card into any color of your choice by discarding a card or sacrificing a worker or two of another color or paying a couple guilders. Either that or have stacks of cards in each of the colors off to the side whose fronts are blank, and you can draw one and only one of these blank cards in any color you choose per round. The game can also induce AP in me and my friends like almost no other game.

RATING: 8.5
Bruges is a fun play.  Be prepared for some randomness, but the whole concept of the draw decks prepare you for that.  The available actions are simple, so there is not much wait between turns.
Choices are still good, and there are combos to he had if you build properly.  Multiple uses for each card always gave you enough choices to keep things going.
There are disasters that can happen, but but I am not sure how I feel about them.  They didn't strike hard in my first play, but the random part of the card draw could make them tougher to deal with.
I liked the scoring.  A little happened in the game, but most happened at the end.  Good tension, and keeps your focus on the task at hand...not worrying about who is 3 points ahead.
Good game.  I think the random nature adds to the replay value.  
Don't forget to avoid first edition until they fix the coins and card fix.

RATING: 8
Fase 1: kaarten aanvullen tot 5 kaarten.

Fase 2: 5 dobbelstenen gooien: dobbelstenen in oplopende volgorde op speelbord leggen. Voor elke gegooide 5 of 6 nemen de spelers een bedreigingsfiche in de kleur van de dobbelsteen. derde fiche van dezelfde kleur: onheil! Daarnaast kan je je pion in het stadhuis een plaatsje gaan opwaarderen. Je betaalt de kosten, berekend door elke gegooide 1 en 2 op te tellen en zet één stapje vooruit.

Fase 3: kaarten uitspelen en bijhorende acties uitvoeren: 
mogelijkheden:
1. twee handlangers nemen in de kleur van de kaart
2. evenveel geld nemen als op de dobbelsteen in de kleur v/d kaart
3. één bedreigingsfiche afleggen in de kleur v/d kaart
4. stukje van de vesten bouwen 
5. een huis bouwen, kaart gedekt neerleggen
6. persoon op een huiskaart leggen

Fase 4: meerderheden uitdelen: de spelers mogen hun meerderhedenfiche omdraaien als ze de meerderheid hebben bij stadhuis, personen of vestenbonus

RATING: 8
Lighter Stefan Feld game where players assist in the development of medieval Bruges.  Some dice are rolled which sets up some parameters for the turn.  Players then take turns playing a card from their hand and taking an action associated with the card.  Actions include drawing income, cash, workers, building canal segments and so on.  The card played may be recruited into that player's faction, granting some special power for the remainder of the game.  The player with the most victory points wins the game.

Bright, colourful game, that plays very smoothly, and is much lighter than Feld's more recent games.  The actions are quick to resolve, and the 150 or so character cards in the keep it quite fresh as you explore character ability combinations.  I enjoyed this one.

RATING: 7
This is the type of game that I think Feld does best.   It's lighter and easy to learn, like Speicherstadt or Notre Dame and not too overwrought with piles of point-scoring mechanisms, like Trajan or Bora Bora.   There's damage mitigation in 5 categories, another Feld hallmark.   Cards serve many purposes, being discarded for workers, money, houses, canals, or threat reduction, but they also contain useful characters that can be slotted into houses, providing instant, one-shot, or repeatable paid abilities.   The actions provided by the character cards can be quite nasty and unexpected, and that gives the game some spice.  There's a hint of CCG here.  At $60 retail, this is most certainly over-priced, but otherwise a quick and fun game with a lot of variety in the cards.

RATING: 9
Kipróbáltuk 3 fővel a játékot. Töredelmesen be kell valljam, hogy nekem nagyon tetszenek a kártya vezérelt euro-k, a kétségtelen szerencsefaktor ellenére vagy épp azért :). S meg kell mondjam, hogy eddig az összes Feld tetszik, amit kipróbáltam, mert ugyanúgy, mint ebben is, több út is vezethet győzelemre, a kártyákat kell ügyesen kombinálni, a kocka nemigazán visz be többlet szerencsét a játékba, közben folyamatosan úgy érzed, hogy "játszol", így vagy úgy, de 'VP'-t (Victory Point) szerzel folyamatosan, a másiknak keresztbe lehet tenni, de még sincs sértődés belőle, egyszóval 'FUN'. Minden forduló 4 fázisból áll, de a harmadikban gondolkodunk a legtöbbet, mire kiválasztjuk a 4 akciónkat, mivel minden kártyával 5 fix akciót végre lehet hajtani, ami mégis sokszor teljesen különböző értéket képvisel helyzettől függően, mivel a kártya színe döntően befolyásolja a használhatóságát. Ezen kívül a 6. lehetséges akció viszi el a játék magját, hiszen a toborzás útján szerezhető szakemberek és azok képességei fogják meghatározni főleg a stratégiánkat. Egy játék után azt mondanám, hogy a játék jól ki van egyensúlyozva a szerencse szerepének figyelembevételével is. Nekem nem sikerült olyan képességű emberkéhez jutni, aki plusz akciót kínál. Azt hittem, így döntően le fogok maradni a többiektől. Annak ellenére, hogy nekem kevesebb emberem volt és közöttük egy sem volt olyan, aki plusz akciót biztosított volna, mégis sikerült a 2. helyen végeznem egy teljesen más stratégiával, 2 ponttal lemaradva az elsőtől. Egyébként a 3. játékos is 2 ponttal volt tőlem lemaradva a végelszámolásnál, így elmondható, hogy ez egy szoros parti volt. A játékban nincs túl sok szabály és elég egyértelműek, így a játék nyugodtan ajánlható családoknak is, akit nem befolyásol a kártyák nyelvfüggősége. Mivel egy-egy kártyalapot helyzettől függően többféleképpen is fel lehet használni, úgy gondolom, hogy a játék újrajátszhatóságával sincs gond. A játékra egy óra játékidő van írva, ami valószínűleg tartható is, mi mégis el húztuk több, mint 2 órán át a játékszabály ismertetéssel együtt, de ez nem mérvadó, mert első játék volt, s mi szeretünk sokat gondolkodni, hogy "megfontolt" lépést tegyünk :). Mindent egybevéve én csak ajánlani tudom. (2015.07.26.)

RATING: N/A
[b][FirstPlayOn10thSept2013][/b] I really like the board. It's small, yet functional in every area. No waste of space, I think.

The game includes 8 overview cards (2 for each player) and 1 setup card. It really helps teaching the game and speed up the process. I always print player aids from the files section of a game, but when I saw this game has them, I was happy. The overview cards explained everything about the game (end game, game phases, all 6 actions and effect of threats) very well.

I also like the interaction in this game, be it the majority tokens, card play and the dice.

I really really dig this game. It's light (to me), yet deep because of the card play. A friend of mine said this is Stefan Feld's take on [thing=19857][/thing], as a card in this game can be used for one of multiple things. Pretty cool.

[b][Update 22nd Sept 2013][/b]: Currently at my 10th play since I got the game 17 days ago (10 plays within 17 days)! That's less than 3 weeks, and less than a month!

[b]Status: Purchased, on 6th September 2013[/b]

RATING: 8.5
Bruges is the third Stefan Feld game of the year. It's not as extraordinary as Bora Bora, which is a truly brilliant thing where the aims and ideas interweave beautifully and with complexity. Nor is it as elegantly simple and streamlined as Rialto, which is an astonishingly good game (and I am loving the 9/10 review for that one in the German Spielbox magazine). 

Instead here we have a kind of Macao-lite (his game from 2009), where the game revolves around character cards and the interplay between the benefits of the cards you play. With 165 different cards there is much to explore here, although the luck of the card draw is fairly dominant. In Macao you feel in control whereas here your strategy and tactics are dictated by your card drawing. Also you can be prone to 'bad dice' here rather than in Macao where you select the dice and manage your tactics around them. I like it, but it isn't on the similar level as Macao nor his other two sumptuous designs from 2013: Rialto and Bora Bora. 

Update: it is growing on me more. I've upped to an 8.

RATING: 8
A card management game played over rounds of four actions each. Players must decide which cards will be played for their abilities (one time or persistent) and which will be fodder for other actions, money and tableau building. There's a vying for leader in three categories that can swing a few points at the end of the game. Between 3 or 4 players struggling to take the lead for these bonus points is very difficult! The expansion rounds out the game wonderfully, after 5 plays I'm convinced the expansion is absolutely necessary. The best thing about this game is the same as In the Year of the Dragon - you have to hang on tight and manage as best you can a strategy out of what the game deals you.

60-90m, best with 3-4 players. 5+ plays

[b]Rating based on - 3 plays, 2 player[/b]
A board game with multi-use cards that you can use to take workers or money, mitigate a threat, build a canal or a house, or hire the character depicted on the card all in order to build up the Renaissance era city of Bruges and score the most victory points. Also played with the boats from the expansion which was super fun but not enough for this to be a keeper.

RATING: 9
Light/medium Feld game, I love games that have cards with multiple uses.  Can be screwed by luck (if you don't draw a card of a colour you need), but I don't mind that in a 45-60 min game, and you must make the best of whatyou get.  

Feels somewhat CCG-like with persons having unique abilities.  Reminds me somewhat of Glory to Rome with multiple uses for each card, and Magic the Gathering with the way that persons are activated by workers (and tapping).  Fairly simple rule-set, lots of options and your game strategy changes radically depending on what persons you have in play.

Perhaps best with 3 or 4, but decent with 2.

Note important changes implemented in [thread=1066510]second printing[/thread] (which I have), especially to the [thread=1104560]engraver[/thread] which was originally [thread=958355]overpowered[/thread].

[thread=1260718]Strategy tips[/thread]

RATING: 10

RATING: 7.5
One of the lighter Feld games. Turns move quickly in this game and the actions are all pretty simple. You build your own little engine with the characters you put down.

However I do not like the take-that cards in this game. In some games I do not mind them, but here one card can destroy your meticulously built engine and destroy your chances of winning. It's too frustrating to put a lot of resources in your engine only to see it destroyed the same turn.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Bruges has turned out to be one of my favorite Feld games.  The basic rules are fairly simple, but there are so may options with 150 unique cards. Many of the familiar Feld elements are there: the many paths to victory, the constant threats that loom over the game, and an ingenious use for dice that are rolled once each turn.  There is significantly more luck in this game than his others with the card draw playing such an important part, but I still feel like I have a lot of control about which cards to use in what ways and there are so many good options that I don't ever feel that unlucky.  It feels a little like Race for the Galaxy meets In the Year of the Dragon.  I enjoyed this game a lot more than I thought I would.  It's only one of two games out of over 120 new games I've tried since January 2013 that I rate 9.5 or higher (the other being Tzolk'in).

RATING: 4
Too slow for me and has too much random to be a true engine builder - you can attempt to create an efficient engine but even with a lot of powers you can end up with two turns and no cards to power them.

The problem, as I see it, is that it's a solitaire with just enough low grade interaction to prevent it from being played simultaneously. Removing some of the take-that interaction and replacing it with end of turn results might make it more simultaneous and faster.

Right now it doesn't clock in at 60 minutes w. 4 players but almost twice that, and that's way, way, way too long for what it delivers.

Great art though, and some fun (or painful) opportunities for screwage.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I was a little surprised to see HiG publish a Feld title. Having played the game now, I no longer am. Now I am just surprised that Brügge is a Feld title. I'll have to render that a little more precisely, though.

In some ways, Brügge is a very typical Feld: There are lots of little ways to score points, a boatload of "stuff that does things" (cards instead of tiles this time), and lots of tactical optimization challenges. In some other ways it's rather atypical: There is a pronounced luck factor determining which cards you get to draw and what catastrophes happen and some direct attack cards as well, although they always target the entire table and never just individual players. Due to the huge number of different cards in the game you can also never be sure that a synergy you are building for and investing in can actually be exploited. If such a plan does come together the benefits can be huge.

All in all the game is far too chaotic and tactical for my liking, or too long and too strongly concerned with minute details that don't matter much in the end.

RATING: 7.5
Very nice game. I love games where you can combine cardpowers and try to find good combos.
There are some negatives with this game:
- There is a high luck factor in the game:
-- the dice define how many disasters come into play and if you have easy acces to money or not.
-- sometimes you don't get the cards of the color you need.
-- building good combo depends on luck. If you play a card which becomes stronger for each other card of a certain type you have in play it can happen that you never see this type again in the game.

So it is much more a game of reaction than planning. If you play a character who helps you to get rid of disastertokens and the whole game you get no 3 of a kind this character was wasted compared to the cards the other players played. If you play this card and there come lot of disasters this card can give you the victory. But you can't know what will happen when you play the card.

I don't like the fact that building a canal and stepping up the reputation track give no ingame advantage. That is just boring compared to playing persons.

RATING: 5
Components: good quality
Complex: easy to teach and learn
Weight: medium- light
Players: 3
Length: a bit more than 60 mins
Opinion: I used to like those games that use cards giving you the opportunity to do lot of things with them, so you have to choose carefully what to do with them and refuse some of the bonuses you have on them. But I had the feeling that there was too much randomness in this game.
You have cards all turns, and draw new ones to replace, choosing the color you have available from 2 different decks. So far, so good, but, at least for me, you have the wrong feeling that you can control what you are doing, while you are depending so much on the cards you draw and the colors available.
Found also a bit unbalance on the cards, if you have the opportunity to have a really good card from the beginning, everything from there is not as difficult as it should.
You could say that I could control things if I would play more, but do not have the feeling nor the necessity that I would do. Will play it if someone put it on the table, but will not suggest it

RATING: 7.5
Bruges is a tightly knit action game based on people cards representing different classes in the medieval city of that name.

The cards are needed to provide money, worker resources, housing for other cards, to build canals and to alleviate threats and/or take actions. Choose the right ones, build at the right times, and you may be the winner of this game.

Of course there is also a variant use for dice in the game (it is a Feld design).  Between dice and card draw, a certain amount of luck is built in- but there are so many options that while choosing can be difficult, there is generally something useful to do so that frustration is minimized.

I found Bruges to be enjoyable, tightly linked to choices selected (as well as any luck-based elements), and over all a nicely thought-out game which is fun to play and rather variable.

Started at a 7.75 after (2) plays.

規則:
*起始運河獎勵版塊高分疊到低分放置中間
*將牌庫分成五疊洗勻，根據人數選同樣疊數洗勻並在分成兩疊放在牌盒中，剩下的放在旁邊
*每個人一個在5分一個在Town Hall，並選一區方guard house標記，並拿五色工人各一跟五塊錢
遊戲每大輪有四個階段
A 抽牌:抽兩個牌庫抽到手牌有五張，可任選，都有任一牌庫抽完，拿放在旁邊的補，該輪為最後一輪(如果使卡片能力導致的則下輪才是最後一輪)
B 擲骰 前進議會軌 : 照數字大小排，五或六的顏色骰分發威脅標記，滿三個就啟動逞罰並棄掉，把1跟2的骰點總合，付對應錢幣可前進一格聲望軌
C 出牌: 輪流出總共要出四張，每張牌有六個動作可選，棄威脅標記會得一分，付跟卡同色一個工人蓋房，有人物功能可在自己回合使用
D 比三個領域:第一個翻面遊戲結束四分，翻正的以後輸也不用翻回來
之後就換手
*最後記分，房子一分，人物分數，人物加分，運河分數，聲望軌分數

RATING: 6.9
Interesting game.
At the start of a round, you draw back up to 5 cards. You can kind of speculate what colors you want (you can see the card backs as you draw), but there really isn't that much choice--essentially you see 5 cards; you must draw 4; one card is from previous turn so there is your 5 cards. Then dice are rolled which determine various things such as how much money you get from cashing in those colors for money that round, etc.

What stinks though is if you NEED a particular color (ie: purple) for the next canal build action, you might just be SOL. Like, you might never actually see a purple card ever. [We had a player who didn't see it any for four rounds straight.] And there isn't anything you can do about that. Eh. So the luck factor in this Feld can be much more hurtful.

RATING: 9.2
[b]2 Player Review:[/b]

It took me awhile to get into the minutia of [i]"Bruges"[/i]. At first it seemed to be an incredibly random game of chance. But after I learned to dedicate myself to one strategy early on in each game, my appreciation for the depth of the game changed. It isn't only about mitigating disasters, it's all about managing your hand efficiently to take advantage of every end game scoring chance your random hand of cards gives you, even if they only appear once every two rounds. 

Don't ever be detoured by an imperfect hand, there is always something meaningful to be done until those end game scoring chances present themselves. A ton of the fun comes from building houses, preparing workers for special ability combos, and putting out 5 different impending dooms. And you`re doing all this while fighting tooth and nail to not give an inch of leeway to the competition. Desperately trying to prevent them from capitalizing on the 3 bonus tiles for being in the lead in 3 different point-scoring fields.

[b]Pros:[/b]

- A very tense game of damage control that plays out in only 45 minutes for 2.
- Lots to think about, not just because each card can be used for many actions, but because each character special abilities allow for a lot of long term strategies. 

[b]Cons:[/b]

- Can feel like a loosing cause sometimes because of all the negative events.
-There are a number of aggressive cards in the deck that could cause some friction. 

[b]2015 Rating:[/b] 9.20 [b]Rank:[/b] 14 [b]Plays:[/b] 3
[b]2014 Rating:[/b] 8.85 [b]Rank:[/b] - [b]Plays:[/b] 9

RATING: 5
If I'm going to play a dry point grabbing Euro game, then it better be one with low luck, that is not the case here. Several cards (2 or 3) are overpowered compared to the rest of the pack, and while there are several copies of them, WHEN and IF you get them is critical to whether or not you will win, and its random (as are the colours you will pick up when drafting which is important, as are the colours of the high numbered dice rolled and will they match your cards also important, nevermind the screw you cards, etc). 

If you get the card that lets you "Pick up a card and do the action" (cost one worker) right near the beginning of the game, every round as long as you have the right colour card to get two workers, you can spend one action to get the workers and then use them to get two actions netting 1 extra action every round. In a game where you are getting 4 actions a round, having 5 instead of 4 is a HUGE advantage especially from the start! In the game I played one player got this early and one got it late and to me it made all the difference. (I see many others complaining about the Engraver as well and is a known issue that Feld changed in newer version)

Now there are some positives here for sure: Quick pace, easy to learn, short game time, several options and multiple ways to score BUT here is another thing I didn't like, the lack of planning. Its almost purely tactical, you don't know what cards you will get, what dice will be rolled, maybe not that important for this but it bugged me anyway.  

I typically like card driven games a lot, and this game might have been a 7 for me (also had a touch of crisis management which I also usually really like) but I have docked it 2 points (should maybe be 3) for being another unoriginal dry generic point grabber and more importantly for having too much luck in such a game. YMMV, hopefully most games the luck evens out, but with so many other games around I'm not sticking around in this one to find out, possibly my least favourite Feld to date.

RATING: 7.9
Bruges is an enjoyable, well-illustrated card-based game that requires a blend of tactical decision making, long-term strategy and player interaction.  While the majority of a player's actions are based on effectively using the cards dealt, the game rewards players who play with a long-term strategy in mind.  As an example, a player has to balance the tactical decisions of which cards to discard for guilders/workers/houses with their long-term Victory Point generation strategy, such as canal building or person recruitment.  

While there is luck in the game, the mechanics grant the player reasonable latitude to mitigate the luck factor.  For instance, players can be penalized from the random results of the dice roll each round (i.e., "threat markers"), but the game provides players with the means to mitigate the impact of the threats through short-term tactics (such as discarding a card to remove a threat or eliminating the impact of the threat by using the threatened resource) or long-term planning (recruiting a person that addresses a threat).  

Finally, the game encourages a healthy amount of player interaction (i.e., managing "majorities" and recruiting persons that can force your opponent to change focus).  Unlike some eurogames, here I feel that I am truly playing against my opponent and not against the game itself.  

My wife and I will be enjoying this relatively quick game for a long time.

Rating: 7.9 (Fun 9; Gameplay 7; Components 9; Theme 4)
Formula:
[Weighted rating = (((Fun*4) + (Gameplay*3) + (Components *2) + (Theme *1))/10)]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a very random game. It can be fun trying to find combos between the powers, and the multi-use cards do allow for a lot of possibilities for your turn. But a lot of the time you just need a card of a certain color, and it's possible you'll never see a card of that color. It's one thing if this is your canal that you can't complete, you could argue that the game is then just pushing you in a different direction and it's your job to react. But if you set up a powerful action combo and you never see any cards that let you get the appropriate colored workers again that can hurt. And the dice are also problematic in that you might just never happen to have cards that can get you a lot of money, or get rid of the pie pieces. But at the end of the day it's a fairly breezy game, so it can be fun to go along for the ride as long as you know there's a lot of luck.

RATING: 6
It's one of those "I want to do everything" games.  The cards do six different things, some of which cost resources (money or workers).  Each player starts with 5 cards and plays them one at a time until everyone has played 4 cards; then everyone replenishes.  Some of the cards stay in play and give you abilities that are one-time, continuing, or pay-to-use.  There's AP potential, but once you figure out your strategy for a turn, it's usually bang-bang-bang.  This is not usually the type of game that I want to play a lot, but I'd certainly play it again.

RATING: 6.5
2013-10-11

Initial Rating: 6.5 (October 2013)

Another cool and different use of dice, another game in which you can do points in many ways, another typical Feld game, which is not a bad thing. I like Bruges, I feel there is a healthy dose of luck involved (mostly with the color and the unbalanced powers of the cards but also with the dice), but you can mitigate it with clever play. I feel this is a combination of Glory to Rome with a boardgame and the result is an elegant, light-medium, Euro game. You can use the cards in 6 different ways and that provides a lot of interesting decisions (highly tactical), however I felt the game lighter than I usually prefer (thus the rating). There is fun tension (another Feld hallmark) via the die catastrophes. 

The game components and art are ok (nothing special), the theme is paper-thin (as usual from Feld), the game can be explained quickly and it moves fluidly so I didn´t felt any downtime (a huge plus for me), still I prefer to play it with 3 but I won’t say no to a 4 player game :D. The game can be very confrontational, with many characters having a nasty/special power that will weaken your position.
 
I prefer Bora Bora over this one but I have yet to play Amerigo and Rialto. For now, I won’t say no to a game of Bruges and perhaps I will buy the game in the near future. I´m rating it a 6.5 because the game is lighter than I expected and it also have lots of luck with the cards.

RATING: 7
Many gamers seem to expect something different from Mr. Feld more in line with his Alea big box releases. Brügge delivers a distinctly tactical experience with some chaos thrown in for good measure that's dividing opinions among gamers. I enjoy it as a lightweight/medium game. Just accept that you can't always get the card colours you want and don't plan for them. This is not what Brügge is about anyway. Make the best out of what you're dealt and decide how much risk you want to take.
A few kinks keep it from being a great game. The bonus tiles are kind of odd. Mostly it's collecting them early or never at all. Some might never be claimed even with high invest when players progress jointly, others might be taken with a single action. The 4 points each of them provide are too important for this to not be bothering. If you're unlucky in card draws or die rolls your chances to win can be diminished easily without a major fault. Considering the game length of about an hour and the fun factor involved I can live with it. I also don't mind the take-that some of the cards introduce as all other players similarly are targeted.
While Brügge will not satisfy heavy eurogamers and strategists, it's an original game that might be worth owning. It could be still more appealing if the drab artwork was less of a deterrent. 
The 2013 Feld ranking: Bora Bora >= Brügge >>> Rialto

RATING: 9
I like Bruges. There are a lot of ways to succeed, a lot of choices each round because the cards in your hands serve multiple purposes. The flow of the game is to be praised: everyone takes 4 actions per round, but they take them one at a time per player, so you don't spend a long time waiting for your turn. It has randomness in the die rolls, but the same dice affect everyone, so you can't get a personally terrible roll. You [i]can[/i] get bad card draws, but even the card drawing has some self-determination to it since there are two face-up piles to draw from. Very good game. Similar to [thing=8217][/thing] in the multi-purpose cards and multiple paths to victory.

출력
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Just played for the first time this past weekend, and really enjoyed it! As a Feld game, I really enjoyed the dynamics involved; the dice reminded me of Macao, while the cards were a nice new addition. There is a bit of random luck involved in the game, because of the dice and the cards, but not so much that you can blame luck for lack of strategy. The dice affect everyone equally, and with the cards, it's about playing them to the best possible effect - kind of like in Agricola.  I played with 4 players and we all enjoyed it. I won by 1 point!

We did all notice a few things, regarding point scoring. Building the canals seems almost not worth the time, as the point yield is very minimal compared to the cards. As a fix for this, I think we might alter the rules slightly so that if a player builds both canals to the "3 point" marker or beyond, then the point value doubles. 12 points at game end would give more of an edge than just 6 (3 points per canal). This might entice players to focus on the canals more.

Other than that, the game play is balanced and fairly quick moving. There's not a lot of player interaction, other than fighting for majority, but players can directly impact each other by playing cards that hurt other players.

I'm not a big fan of card-based games, like Dominion and Race for the Galaxy, but this one provides a nice mix of cards, a board, and meeples that provide a more "board game" feel, despite being card-driven.

Another Feld success!

RATING: 7
This is a solid Feld design that is very tactical in nature where you do the best with what you have in your immediate hand.  With all the 130+ cards being unique, one never exactly knows what one will draw/do.   The game plays in just around an hour if everyone knows how to play and is enjoyable for that time frame even with the luck of the draw.  The aspect of gold for hiring people cards and then acquiring workers to take special actions on some of the people cards works well to the point where there is basically two currencies in the game.  

There is potential for nastiness/take that in this game with one set of people cards which can be very effective if played at the right time.  The variance of the people cards in worker based effects, instant one time effects, end game scoring effects, etc. adds quite a bit of spice to the game as well.  There can be some agony/frustration with the card draw if you can't draw a certain color ever...there has been a couple games where I couldn't draw a brown card to save my life (I had two all game long).

RATING: 9
A solid, fast-playing design from Stefan Feld, and possibly the only game I own that has dice, cards, AND meeples!

The game is packed full of difficult decisions, with each card offering multiple choices, only one of which can be taken. This makes for a delightfully agonizing game. The game plays reasonably quickly, and is a good medium weight game typical of what I've come to expect from Hans im Gluck.

Artwork and components are fantastic. There's 165 unique cards, each with unique artwork (so lots of replayability as well as great art).

RATING: 8
Light card driven Feld game. The material (board and cards) really gets you into the atmosphere of Brugge. Still it stays pretty abstract. 

Once you know the game, it plays very fast. The cards are not always very clear in their descriptions.
The artwork of the game makes this a fun game to play.

Luck is important in this game. Which cards you draw at what time can influence the game enormously, especially when the game is short (f.e. 2 player game)

Main activity should be building houses and playing persons. 
The dice, can screw up your plans, but they are applicable to all players.
It is a fact that some cards seem a bit over powered, so getting and playing a strong card in the beginning of the game, can make a huge difference.

The gameplay is lacking a theme. You don't really know what you are doing, except trying to score points in different unrelated ways. There is no real theme related goal. No narrative. That is a pity and not uncommon for Feld games. 

Luckily the colorful board, the nice drawings of real Brugge buildings, the many different cards make this game attractive to play. 

Make the best of what you get. If you can live with that, you should try Bruges.

Minus: 2 (i miss the link between the gameplay and the theme) - 9 (too abstract for beginner)

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Fun game.  I enjoy these types of games where there are multiple paths to victory, and you get variable powers.  It's fun to get an ability that nobody else has, and there are TONS of cards in this game with all sorts of different such abilities.

I'm really impressed that there are 165 different character cards, EACH with their own unique art work.  Compare that to Macao, which also has several different characters, but each one is illustrated with the same bearded man.  Even "Mrs. Red", "Lady Black", and "Baronessa Um" are all illustrated with this bearded man :) - so yes, Bruges much better in that regard!  

Colorful game too.  I really enjoy the 5 different colors, with their different options, corresponding dice, workers, threats, etc..

Only thing that seems a bit jarring and out of place is the "meanie" cards.  They can really hurt people quite a bit depending on their timing.  Feels like building a card house and someone bumping the table.  Seems a bit out of place.  I think they made the red cards potentially too harmful.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
As usual with Stefan Feld this game is tighter than a camel's a*** in a sandstorm. Everything is related to everything else, dependant on everything else and can completely bugger up your plans like everything else. Throw on top of this tight system the luck of the draw from the card deck and you have what many have called an unfair card driven luck-fest.

I disagree. I really like this game. It's not my favorite Feld game, have a look at my comments on Bora Bora for those. In Bruges I love the way it uses dice to set the economic / disaster regime of each round. I love the way you can use each card for 6 different things and that the role of the dice will set the value of many of these uses. I love that it has a little bit of interaction between players and most of all I love that it is fairy quick. If it was a very long game I would agree with most of the points detractors make about this game. But at the length it is, it is fun, thinky and competitive.

I haven't tried the expansion, I don't play the base game enough. Something I would like to change. Definitely a keeper until my kids get older and I can hopefully get them to enjoy it. I have a feeling I just have to talk up the fire, floods, plague of rats etc that you can send someone else's way and they will love it!

RATING: 6
I am a sucker for Multiple-Uses-For-Cards games, but after playing this game a few times I have no desire to actually own it myself.

I really like that each card has a special character which you can build up to in order to progress your town.  That mechanic works very nicely.  However, what I don't like is that the dice can feel a bit tacked on to the game.  Their roll can determine whether the roles in your hand are worthwhile or not, which is extremely frustrating.  A good hand is one with many different cards, but you also want to look for synergistic sets.  It's just.. weird.

I would probably like, and think about owning, this game if I didn't already own Race for the Galaxy and had played Innovation and Glory to Rome.  I would recommend all three of those games over this one.

RATING: 7.5
Feld does Glory to Rome.  There is a lot to love about this little game, but it could be better with a little less randomness on the card draw.  I really enjoy it as a 2p game, and I assume that is probably the best player count.  Especially with the majority tiles, it can make for some interesting battles/decisions.  Throughout the game, there are constant decisions where you can't do everything you want with the cards.  There are a lot of strategies to pursue, but it's tricky to settle on one from the get because you don't know how the cards are going to fall.  I almost wish there were different sets of pre-selected decks that you could mix in so that players had a little better idea of what cards/strategies are available. 

Still a fun game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Bruges is a game of "here's what you get, now make the best of it." You draft cards each round only knowing their color. From there, you try to maximize your return by choosing one of six possible actions for 4/5 of your cards (the final card is carried over to the next round, typically). The game can be frustrating if you can't catch a color you really need, or if your characters don't work well together. The best player will not always win, but will win more often than others. Bruges is much like poker in that respect.

**********

If you buy the expansion, I recommend using just the boats.  They allow for a wider range of decisions and a little more forward planning without changing the overall feel of the game.

RATING: 6
Downgraded from a 9 after a 4 player game. My original comments still stand for a 2 player game, as does my original rating, until I can play again with 2 and see if the same issues hold. In the 4 player game, it seemed that the luck of what cards and which people you drew had a drastic impact on how well you did. Some abilities seem much more powerful than others. The abilities that relied on collecting people from certain professions were next to useless in a four player game, perhaps because those professions were split between four people rather than two? The winner was clear a few rounds before the last round was triggered. Definitely not as enjoyable an experience, and it seemed much more dependent on luck than on how you chose to play your cards. More plays necessary to figure out if this is an issue with four players or with the game as a whole. - rating of 6 based on this experience.

Bought at GenCon 2014

Previous comments:
Something about this game really clicks with me. It is easy to learn, has streamlined play and still makes for interesting strategies (which can't be said for all Feld games, especially the first two). It also plays really well with 2 players, and I love it for that.

I love how the colours of the dice, cards, workers and people interact. Do you use that blue card to get 6 coins while the blue dice is a 6 this round, do you use it to get rid of a threat token, or do you use it to activate your abilities? Weighing up the choices based on the restrictions the cards place on you is very interesting.
-Rating of 9 based on 2 player experience

RATING: 6
This is light for a Feld but it's a great game. The multiple uses for cards and the variety of person abilities gives it a Glory to Rome or Race for the Galaxy feel but certainly less thinky. The trademark Feld disasters are present and ever-looming, but can be mitigated and often not harmful (and occasionally even beneficial!). It's shorter and lighter, easier to teach and easier to get to the table. Frustrations can occur when you can't draw the color you need but dealing with what you're dealt is all part of the fun.

After many plays, downrating from 8 to 6. It's a fine game but it just doesn't bring that much excitement anymore. Probably a bit too random to really capture me.

RATING: 9
Second hand 

Rules are very straight forward. It's a typical Feld game but one of the easier ones (together with La Isla). I think it's just perfect: a lot of different ways to gain points, plays in less than 2 hours (including reading the rules) with 4 players. There is some player interaction but also a lot of solitaire play: digging canals, building houses, attracting people to your city etc. I really liked it and I would love to play it again. Feld makes clever use of the dice again in this design.

RATING: 8.5
Brügge, die Stadt in Flandern (Belgien) die aussieht, "wie aus einem beschissenen Märchen". Wer das Zitat erkannt hat, der kennt auch den großartigen Film "Brügge sehen und sterben". Ein ungewöhnlicher Film voll mit schwarzem Humor und Tarantino-würdigen Dialogen. Doch was hat das alles mit dem Spiel zu tun? Nun, der Film war für mich quasi Auslöser für die Faszination der Stadt Brügge. Zweimal war ich bereits dort und kann es Jedem nur wärmstens empfehlen. Natürlich spricht mit demnach auch das Szenario des Brettspiels zu 100% an. Wobei die Stadt Brügge hier eigentlich nur Mittel zum Zweck ist, denn eigentlich stehen die mittelalterlichen Stadtbewohner mit Mittelpunkt. Durch Karten werden diese Personen dargestellt und hierbei ist jede Person einzigartig und kann auf 5 unterschiedliche Arten genutzt und eingesetzt werden. 1. Geld sammeln 2. Handlanger rekrutieren 3. Bedrohung vermindern 4. Den Kanal ausbauen 5. Ein Haus bauen und schlussendlich eben diesen Charakter in das Haus einziehen lassen. Brügge liefert sehr viele Strategien und ist wirklich Variantenreich. Ja, es ist auch ein etwas Glück im Spiel (ziehe ich die richtige Karte, welche Würfel wurden gewürfelt) und ja, es ist auch etwas destruktiv, aber das macht mir bei Brügge nix aus. Es ist ein tolles Spiel!

RATING: 8
Like Macao and Amerigo, this feels like a 'risk mitigation' game by Feld. Of course there's randomness with the die rolls and card draws, but I suspect one of the important strategies is to set yourself up so that you can always do something productive regardless of which cards come up or what die rolls occur. In my first game, I did a poor job of that -- I was heavily focused on the blue die and blue cards, and when the blue die rolls were poor and no blue cards were available for a couple of turns, I was toast.

Overall there's nothing groundbreaking here -- and I like Macao and Amerigo more -- but Bruges is still fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Each round, players first draw five colored cards from two stacks. Five colors of dice are rolled, which tell you what you can do with those cards. On a player's turn, they can sell a card for gold (corresponding to the die rolled for that color), recruit 2 workers of that color,  build a canal of that color, build a house of that color, hire a person, or discard a threat. 

Based on the dice rolls, you also have to decide if you want to spend money to go up on influence. You also have to compete for majority in canals, people and influence. The twist is once you have gotten the majority, you can never lose those points even when someone surpasses you later in the game. 

The theme of the game is based on the Belgain town of Bruges. This Stefan Feld game is very tense, and engaging, with a myriad of possibilities. There are 166 person cards, so there is certainly no lack of variety.

I've really enjoyed my playings of this so far. It's hard to follow a particular script, and the different characters in the game might change how you play. I'd like to get many more plays under my belt to feel comfortable with the range of characters. There are many different approaches to the game as well, and want to play more to get exprienced with the strategies. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Feld
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Hmm. Played a couple times and for the most part, I like how multiuse card play is pretty interesting. I feel like with 2, it lacks something...it either feels exceptionally short or some cards lose even more power and it becomes moreso zero sum and not as interesting. So Id prefer this at 3 and 4. There can be a bit of downtime at 4 though. I also feel like card draw is decently luck based. But I like the design and principles of the game and found it interesting for the most part.

After at least 5 plays, I have some issues with the game. The color of some cards and workers is a little difficult to tell and it annoys me. Also, its multiuse cards in the sense that each card does different things...but each card does the same different things except one effect. I dont know how much I like this as a lot of them can be very weak or not interesting (like get 2 workers which feels comparatively weak). I still prefer this to other games' cards like San Juan which only do about two or three similar tings but I dont know how much I like it. I esp dislike the two workers effect because I dislike using workers to activate the hired peoples. For one, Id prefer resources thematically, and for another, the infinity effects tend to be better long term than worker activations imo. The canal strategy also isnt that interesting pointwise.

The biggest issue is the luck of the draw and lack of coherent strategy. Your opening hand and every few turns initially will determine whether youll even get a strategy rolling or youll just need to rely on tactically drawing hands. Drawing the right colors is important too but thats a fair bit of chance if youll draw the right ones you need for your turn. And even then the abilities may not mesh when you do.

The main things I want in this game are: more interesting ways to multiuse the cards and a way to get a decent strategy going/not rely so heavily on card draw. I prefer startegic games, not tactical.

In addition, the coloration and worker colors is very annoying at times.

RATING: 8
The wealth of options that this card-driven game gives you is astounding...and bit overwhelming, at first.

Every card has a character with a unique ability on it, one of five colors, and the ability to use five different generic abilities that are dependent on the color it is.  This can confuse a new player because there are so many unique abilities that will wind up just being used for their generic ability.

You also can chase points in a few different ways, canals, the prestige track, and the cards themselves.

The game really is fun once you can wrap your head around the possibilities.

RATING: 6

Buildings and different people are represented by colored cards, loads of them that give this game its variety and flavor. The luck factor is mitigated by fast play.

RATING: 3
Played the game a few weeks ago and was unimpressed, especially so with the canals which felt really unconnected and tacked on. Played it again last night with the new expansion. This time I enjoyed it much more as it integrated the canals nicely into rest of the game and set up nice feldian combos.

However, after two and a half hours I set myself up for a massive 11 point last turn which would have easily won me the game and was thwarted by another player randomly "drawing and immediately playing" a take-that character card into his house which took away my worker and screwed me over. These cards have no place in a Feld. Maybe if it were a shorter game, but not in something this long and intricate. It wouldn't have been so bad if he would have blindly drawn a card that randomly gave him enough points to win, which is also possible unfortunately, it was that the card let him destroy my engine that I painstakingly set up. Frustrating experience.

(It's like playing one of Feld's better games except other players can reach over from time to time and move your pieces around and/or take them.)

RATING: 4
I really want to like this game more than I do.  I normally love engine building games and cards having multiple uses. RATING: 8.7
Finally....After Macao this game could get my second 10. Waited soooo long. Just because of the artwork it deserves it. Now after my first play I will keep with the 10. It has a high replayability just because of the 165 cards wich are (as in Macao) not used in every game. I love that!

Edit: After 8 games I rated it down to 8.7. The game is still an excellent piece of replayability and artwork. But it has an issue that let it become short off a 9: The open card deck lets following cards shift while drawing from the deck and opens the possibility of having a glance of what cards might follo. Depending on how much you can see, it influences the gameplay. And if someone wants to cheat, he can do...

As of Mai 2017 (32 games played!) I rated it back a 8.7  after it slowly slipped down to a 8.5. The publishers solved the problem with the shifting cards by preparing card holders, which were easy to receive just by asking them. Now every game contains them. Well done.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.25
心得:
隨機性有點強
但也不是完全沒辦法控制
節奏挺輕快的遊戲
主要是卡牌取捨的遊戲
前期如果能雇到效果不錯的人
遊戲回合數多就會有優勢
規則:
*起始運河獎勵版塊高分疊到低分放置中間
*將牌庫分成五疊洗勻，根據人數選同樣疊數洗勻並在分成兩疊放在牌盒中，剩下的放在旁邊
*每個人一個在5分一個在Town Hall，並選一區方guard house標記，並拿五色工人各一跟五塊錢
遊戲每大輪有四個階段
A 抽牌:抽兩個牌庫抽到手牌有五張，可任選，都有任一牌庫抽完，拿放在旁邊的補，該輪為最後一輪(如果使卡片能力導致的則下輪才是最後一輪)
B 擲骰 前進議會軌 : 照數字大小排，五或六的顏色骰分發威脅標記，滿三個就啟動逞罰並棄掉，把1跟2的骰點總合，付對應錢幣可前進一格聲望軌
C 出牌: 輪流出總共要出四張，每張牌有六個動作可選
1.棄對應顏色威脅標記會得一分
2.付跟卡同色一個工人蓋房
3.付錢雇人到房中，有人物功能可在自己回合使用
4.拿同卡顏色工人兩個
5.付錢蓋同卡片顏色運河
6.對應場上同顏色骰子拿錢
D 比三個領域:第一個翻面遊戲結束四分，翻正的以後輸也不用翻回來
之後就換手
*最後記分，房子一分，人物分數，人物加分，運河分數，聲望軌分數
