RATING: 8
A really nice, clever, elegant Euro with enough luck to move it out of sterile territory and into a fun space - enough to warrant groans, cheers and risk management but generally without being overwhelming ... maybe. Draw cards over 5 colours which allow you to do a variety of things on your turn, and which will change depend on the number rolled in each of the 5 colours. If yellow's a 5, now's the time maybe to trade it that yellow card for 5 gold rather than to acquire 2 yellow meeples, which can be used to activate a yellow card ability on one of my buildings. Or maybe spend it on the next canal building space which requires a yellow card. Or build a yellow house in which I can later build a card power. And so on, using the cards you drew at the start. There's lots of choices which vary throughout, and every turn has a different twist dependent on the dice rolled and the cards that were on offer when you drew from the two decks available. Clean rules, easy to teach, good game length. Lots of different card power combos to explore. You can have a strategy, but it'll require tactical decisions each turn on whether to progress it or change direction. I liked it a lot.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Bruges is best described as a card combo game, though that doesn't really do it justice.  You're trying to get out cards through a multi-step process, and those cards have text that can allow you to do a variety of different things.  Meanwhile, you're trying to build canals for points, advance on a track for end-game points, and get rid of tokens that will harm you if they build up too much.  Everyone basically has the same options, so sometimes you feel like you need to do certain things just to keep up with your opponents.  The randomness factor is a little high because of card draws.  It doesn't really feel like anything else I've played.

(3 plays)

RATING: 5
1 play; Rating: [bgcolor=#9999FF][s][color=#9999FF]-[/color][/s][b]6[/b][s][color=#9999FF]-[/color][/s][/bgcolor]

This comes after a single play, so take everything here with a grain of salt. Bruge is a card game about building canals. The clever part of the game is that each card allows a player to take up to 7 different actions. Most of these are resource-gathering actions of some sort, but a player can also play the card into his own tableau as a person, which gives the player some sort of special ability. This may be a one time effect, an ongoing action, or a discount on future actions. There are many different cards and a major focus of the game is creating interesting combos of cards and using that synergy to gain an edge. However, this is also where the game fell a little flat for me. The combos seem quite basic, and there is little a player can do to search for cards that fit well with a given strategy. And in the end, players are mostly earning points from (1) having built the canals by spending money and playing the appropriate card; (2) advancing on the reputation track by paying a randomly determined amount of money; and (3) playing cards into their tableau. 

I think here Feld goes away from the pattern of scoring points for everything that we've seen in his recent games. The trouble is, in Bruge we're score points for only a few things, and the scoring is done in such a straightforward manner that it feels rather dull. I'm sure I'd enjoy a few more plays in order to explore other card combos, but overall, this is a pass for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A little lighter than most Feld games, but fans will recognize some trademarks (reputation track, threats to contend with).

Basically a card game in which each card can be used for several purposes. The cards are of 6 different colors and those colors are visible on the inverse as well. You must draw a hand of 5 at the beginning of each round from 2 stacks -- selecting the color card that you want from the two available top cards. The draw is done before 6 dice bearing the same colors as the cards are rolled to determine which colors are threats (and can be mitigated by the same color card) and how much each color is worth in money this round. Basically, it's a crap shoot. You have no way of knowing which cards are the most valuable until after you select them and the dice are rolled.

As a result, this leans more towards family than gamers game and more towards tactical than strategic. It's still an enjoyable puzzle to optimize your hand; just don't expect anything orbit altering.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I like how the cards have multiple uses depending on what you want to do, and how their effects can stack and build on each other.

The setup process is frustrating as you take a big stack of cards, split it into five even piles and then two of those piles are your draw decks. 

The reputation track mechanic isn't well thought out as it can be impossible to gain the lead from someone. This is troublesome because it's worth points inherently but also it gives one of the three 'majority bonuses' for the player in the lead.

In the game there are disasters that can wipe away your progress, and the disasters are assigned randomly. Once you get your third of a kind, you suffer the negative effect. There is an in game threat mitigation mechanic but it relies on you having the right colored card in hand at the right time, and since you can only hold 1 card between rounds, this can be difficult. Additionally, not all the disasters are of equal severity.

Some of the personalities are too powerful. In particular the 12 cost cards who are the most expensive guys in the game, have very unbalancing effects.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:nostar: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:nostar: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
My wife likes this game and I am still to beat her at it. 

However, this "Point-salad, Play for an hour and a half to see at the very end who scored a couple points more than the others" mechanics is apparently not for me. Take my opinion with a grain of salt, though, because I also think Stephan Feld's other hit game - Castles of Burgundy is a mediocre game too.

Update: At the third play, I started to grasp the fine mechanics of this game. The card play is where this game is at, specifically in the nobles you put out. It is, at its core, an engine building game where you have to juggle 6 currencies (gouldens and 5 colours of workers, which really are a kind of currency each). That and the fact that you normally play all your cards but one drives me to the edge of AP, although I am not normally known to suffer AP.

The fact, that player interaction is almost non-existent and that the game seems to normally end with an unexciting difference of a couple of points for the winner to show for his/her cunning play is what still makes this game somewhat of a "meh" for us. 

This game gives me a similar vibe to Guildhall - a favourite of ours, but what Brügge offers in terms of clever-combo card gameplay during an entire game, Guildhall does in each round while also having that confrontational edge both my wife and me enjoy.

Played 3 times. Almost a year after my last play, I feel no desire to ever play it again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Despite the vast number of choices you'll be presented in Bruges, the game is really rather simple. Gameplay boils down to dice and cards of matching colors being played or laid out to create powerful combos. All the while, players will be racing to hold majority in a handful of titles throughout the game and racing to be first to finish their canals. It's mostly a game of reacting to your neighbors play and doing the same thing, but better. Teaching the game is a breeze thanks to some very smart design choices.
That being said, I think this is one of Feld's weaker titles. It could be very strong, if not for a handful of overpowered cards. These particular cards don't even require smart play or specific combos to pull off, you simply have to be lucky enough to draw them.
Still, I enjoy playing Bruges. I'm hoping future expansions will fix the balance issues.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I've not played this very many times, and never with 4 (once with 2, the other 3-4 times with 3), but my overall impression each time has been disappointment. It may grow on me with more plays, but I'm not sure when they'll happen, given how many other new and newish games we have that I'm far more excited to play. I'm a big Feld fan and also very keen on card games, so this one sounded brilliant - yet I found it fell somewhat flat in play. I think a large part of the problem is that it's so short - I've nothing against short games, provided they have plenty of strategy, and this game isn't particularly lacking there, but it does mean that you've got very little time to set up any kind of "engine" of tableau cards to generate VPs. The game seems to end before you've really got going - I realise this is a feature of the game and one that players have to adapt to, but I find it a bit jarring here. And the game, while definitely good, doesn't massively engage me, for whatever reason. Still, it provides interesting choices, and with 165 unique cards (albeit with many having similar effects to each other) is not lacking in replayability. It's a good game - hence the 7 rating - but I just feel that I was expecting an even better one.

RATING: 5
Edit: subsequent plays have confirmed my starting idea: this is not a game that allow you to pick a strategy and focus on it, but it's instead a game of limited resources that will force you to try and get the best you can from every hand. It's definetely not a game for those who likes games with low or no luck. But once you accept that you can be easily screwed by dices or hands, however, you can play it more consciously, and get better results.

-------------------

This game is a fine demonstration of how design can go awry even when the premises are good. Why in the world, in a game that already got:

1. A mostly unused board in spite of being sufficiently large to possibly host more things
2. Dices
3. Workers and coins as resources
4. Lot of components, pawns, tokens and the like

...i have to use cards to do absolutely EVERYTHING? Why i can't just have a "merchant" token or the like that i can move on that board to do actions like getting money, hire workers, getting reputation, build canals and so on, while saving cards for the houses/citizens mini game (which is easily the best part of the game and more than often perfectly sufficient to get a win)? Having cards which usage is so overloaded would make sense if the game would consist of almost cards only, but heck, there are tons of other things here anyway. Just give them an use that is as much as interesting as characters are. Most often then not, the entire game revolves about getting (almost by chance only) a deadly combination of characters and other cards of the right colors to get the other resources you need to put them into play. Even if the other mechanics can allow other players to keep up with points, they are so dull and uninspired (expecially the reputation one) that is just natural for a player to ignore the board and focus on the houses/citizens thing.

Trajan, as a Stefan Feld game where you have a hand of cards but only as a part of the mini game dedicated to them, it's a way better game, more cohesive, and with more interaction between players.

RATING: 7
I very much like the simplicity of this game, from the rules to the quick turns to the five easy actions and straightforward people abilities. Everything is streamlined and the points salad doesn't really hit until the final reckoning. Bruges is a lovely backdrop for the game. The people cards are just varied enough to prevent the game from getting boring, and you play few enough and see enough that it feels like you have some meaningful choices.

The reality is that I'm not sure the choices are as meaningful as they first appear. Everything is so balanced that no matter which direction you take, players from a wide range of abilities always end up within a few points of each other. It might be that there are good moves and a few very obvious suboptimal moves that it's just hard to make a bad move.

Anyway, the ride is interesting enough to make this a family game that will see repeated plays and repeated requests to the table. It's very light, very much a Feld salad, but interesting enough to make you want to explore the cards over a few plays.

RATING: 1
Local Feldian decided that this is the best attempt of me liking a Feld. And he was right: Feld on cards is Feld I can tolerate. Even enjoy. 

It's a tableau building game crossed with some old eurogame stuff, like additional mechanisms and ways to get points the game could live without (canals? that thingy in the middle of the board? the "goal" tiles?). The card synergy is rather simple - yes each card has multiple uses which makes the game feel like a colour coded spread sheet cut into cards and shuffled. The biggest lack is a sort of card drawing engine. 

But okay - mostly you'll build your own tableau and see what you can do in any given game. Very MPS. The biggest problem I have with the game ain't even about Feld. It's Metzel's uninspired generic artwork dipped in much too many layers of brown. Seasons still looks better, yet Bruges has more theme (well Seasons really has none) - you're building your city district and that's that. 

As far as characters on cards go, I liked Dominare's ones with their ethnic names and flavour text better. 

Rating down to 1.0 because: uninspired. Yeah it's a crime.  Visuals have a kinda comfy feel, but doesn't save the lack of "why does this game exist at all" problem. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

RATING: 6
Played thrice; once with 4P and twice with 3P. Bruges is entertaining in a typical Feld-like way where you are pulled in several directions at once – should you build canals, hire people, obtain workers, earn money or dispel threats? There are also elements of racing and area majorities wedged in for good measure.

As a fairly light design, Bruges is a generally satisfactory tactical game. Luck of the draw is central to this game, but there is a skill to working with the cards you have been dealt with. (It's also a skill I'm completely missing out on.) Unlike most Feld games, Bruges doesn't have a 'hook' or a mechanism that really stands out.

布魯日

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Clearly suffers from hundreds-of-different-cards-syndrome. It's a game for people who want to have the feeling that they played an immensely complex game, when in reality they only played a game with immensely complex text and rules to hide the fact how random it all is.

In other words: Don't even bother trying to find a good strategy, because you won't get the right cards for it anyway. And that you can keep only one card between rounds makes planning ahead even more pointless. (Which, I assume, is a clever ruse so that players don't need to *actually* think too much and can spend more time flattering their egos for having understood the rules.)

Every game has a target group, and I guess there are many people who will like this game. Good for them. I'm just not among that group. (In fact, I actually enjoyed the first few rounds of the game we played, until I reached that point where I wanted to go for a certain strategy and realized that there was nothing I could do to get the cards I needed. The cards you get basically determine your strategy.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

Feld tavalyi játéka kellemes meglepetés volt számomra. Egy könnyed kártyás játék. Minden körben 5 kártyából indulva 4-et használunk fel különböző akciókra. Emberszerzésre, pénzszerzésre, katasztrófajelző eldobásra, csatornaépítésre vagy házépítésre. Emberre a házépítésre van szükségünk. Egy kártya egyik oldalán egy mesterember van egy jó tulajdonsággal, a másikon pedig egy ház. Így a kártyák sokfelhasználásúak. Pénz az emberek házba tevésére kell, illetve csatornaépítésre. A katasztrófajelzőből 3 kijövése után ér minket valami negatívum, ez a Notre Dame patkányinváziójának itteni megoldása. 175 különböző kártya van jó és kevésbé jó tulajdonságú emberekkel. A játék tulajdonképpen arról szól, hogy ezeket az embereket hogy menedzseljük. Ez a kártyás rész pedig a Macaóból köszön vissza. A játék abszolút nem bonyolult, a kártyák ismerete után gyorsan pörgethető és valószínűleg minden játékosszámmal jól működik. Kimondottan tetszett. Egyedül a kártyák közti különbségeket éreztem néha erősnek, tehát szerencsefaktor bőven van benne, de mivel nem hosszú és nem is nehéz a játék szerintem arányos ehhez.

RATING: 5
I think I underestimated the length of time that I had to get things done, as there are some serious race elements to this in order to get majorities. The game certainly has a luck element with how the dice come up, although I think that can be mitigated with creating a diverse set of colors and how you ultimately decide to use those colors.

RATING: 6.5
It's another Feldian game, with plenty of ways to score, but this is on the lighter, faster, more tactical end of the scale. It's lighter than I prefer -- I would much rather play his other longer, heavier games -- but it's pretty solid for it's play time.

A significant part of the game is managing the cards: without the right cards, you're going to have a hard time accomplishing anything! The cards come in 5 colors, and are people that allow you to do stuff (use colored workers to do something, earn coins or VP or something, etc.). The cards are available for drafting from 2 face up decks: you can see the topmost cards from both decks. You need appropriately colored cards to do the various things in the game: build canals, earn like-colored workers, advance on a VP track, etc. The cards can be placed face down as buildings (using those workers), or played face up onto previously placed buildings -- at which point you can use the varied character powers. 

Sadly, I don't remember more details. There was some kind of catastrophe thing where you could see it coming, there were dice you rolled which set the prices of each of the colored cards (and advanced a catastrophe), there was a track that everyone wanted to move forward on ... it all worked well and I'd like to try it again, but given the choice I'd go for a longer game.

RATING: 8.2

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
2-4, 60m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a very Feld-ian game.  It includes a fun and unique dice mechanic and a plethora of different ways to score victory points.  The only thing it is missing is a brutally punishing personality.  This game is actually very colorful and feels reasonably light-hearted.

The game consists of drawing cards of 5 different colors and taking one of 6 different actions with those cards.  Like I said, in common Feld fashion all of these actions are in service to scoring you points in different areas.  The most versatile and, for me, best part of the game are the different people that appear on each of the cards.  Each one of them has a different special ability and where 90% of the game "lives".  I'm a sucker for variety and variability in a game and think this gives the game a LOT of replay value.  There are combos and interesting tactics to take with the different people cards.

There is an interesting decision mechanic built into the way cards are played as well.  Each round you have to use 4 of your cards and you simply won't be able to play all the people you like to because only 1 of your 5 cards carries over the next turn.  The planning on which of the 6 actions to take with the cards is reasonably deep, thoughtful and for me, quite a bit of fun.  The game is also going to feel very different every time you play and you can take varying paths to victory.

I will say that this game requires a few "learning plays" as I like to call them to really get to the meat of it.  With so much variety and so many different actions to take that score you points in so many ways it can get a little overwhelming and the best plays can be hard to see until you've worked out how exactly everything fits together.  Despite that, this is now one of my favorite Feld games and definitely one that can stand a lot of mileage.

I'd also like to point out that it plays at a reasonably good clip as well, under an hour including setup and explanation.

RATING: 6
This is exactly how I imagined Belgium to be: full of people in houses, awash in canal construction, and with a high value placed upon personal influence.  (Or was that Holland I was thinking of?)  The only thing lacking was some of that famous (righfully!) Belgian beer.  So regarding game play, it's funny that I played Spyrium just a few days before playing this one because my comments for Spyrium could apply to Bruges: it's a very cleverly designed game that's as dry as a Dutchman.  The manner in which the dice and cards interact is brilliant (though I do wonder if designers of Euro-style games are competing to see who can design the game with the most number of ways to use a card).  It IS highly interactive for a Euro game, though I find it to be a frustrating sort of interaction as other players are constantly working toward preventing you meeting your goals (and vice versa, of course).  All in all, it's a great bit of game design that isn't a great amount of fun to play (for fans of thematic, narrative-rich games such as myself).  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 2/5  Total: 6/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Feldian point-salad take on engine-building card games that's as frustratingly milquetoast as I should have expected.

Each round players draw up to five cards, of which four will be played; all cards have common uses (money, build canals, etc.) and, crucially, have a unique power. Some powers are persistent ("get 2 coins every time another player plays a person"), some one-off ("get 2 coins for every person you have in play") and some give end-game points ("2 points for every protector symbol"). There is a tiny amount of direct competition (majority bonuses) and a modicum of interaction (some cards steal from, leech from or otherwise inflict pain on opponents).

Card luck seems substantial: strong "engine" cards early in the game give a compounding advantage, while set-collection bonus cards are all but impossible to plan for.

This is frustrating, because while the game plays smoothly, it has all the drama of my own pleasant day-trip to Bruges, from the highlights (a peaceful beer tasting) to the crises (a rented bicycle that broke down and required a sunny, 10-minute canal-side walk back to the station). "Combo" sounds like too grand a term for the synergies on display, which are more about balanced-to-within-an-inch-of-their-life incremental advantage than the cavalcade of game-breakers that make up a game like Innovation.

There's nothing wrong with Bruges, but it's a lacklustre entry in a crowded field. I have never appreciated Race for the Galaxy more.

**

Update: No, there's something wrong with Bruges. Luck of the cards can be overwhelming and I see no reason to ever play it over the (vastly superior) competition.

RATING: 7.5
A new Feld! And it's from Hans im Glueck! Yay! Too bad only the German is available at the moment, because there is a lot of German text on the cards. But with some sheets with translations next to us, we managed to play this. It did add some time to the length as looking up each and every card took some time.

The game (like most Felds) seems to offer quite some strategies to try out, but the two players who used the cards most for the characters, where in the lead by a good margin, so judging from this first game, it seems like you can't ignore those.

I quite liked this first play and will be more than happy to play this again soon. And I'll probably add it to the collection at some point.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.8
I appreciate the depth of strategy in Bruges, and while simple it offers a complexity of tactical decisions and critical choices. It is a very, very tight game, particularly if the cards don't go your way. I'm not a big fan of the card draw system as theoretically it can essentially hose a player, devastatingly so, and is quite random with minimal control. But Bruges is all about planning, and planning for the worst. Tough decisions need to made, and plenty of sacrifices; will you cover all your bases, or take a risk? The random elements need to be beaten with careful preparation, and there are so many elements to juggle. For a simple game it can bring mind numbing AP, but I love it. It yearns for me to conquer it.

RATING: 7
I enjoyed Bruges although I'm not sure if it's deep enough to hold my attention for the long term. It felt quite a bit like Feld's earlier design Macao, but cleaner, more streamlined and with far better graphics. It's also shorter, with the downside being that all card draws are random, so you can't plan ahead as you can, at least a little, in Macao. A lot I like here, but random luck can really hose your game. The biggest examples of this are 1) You play a character who does something neat when you spend meeples of a certain color, but then you are never able to draw a card of that color to get the requisite meeples, or 2) You play a character who does something neat when you get a good die roll of a specific color, and that color never rolls a high number over the course of the game. Need to play this more to see where my rating finally shakes out, but definitely some mixed feelings here ...

Very interesting game -- Stefan Feld's take on Glory to Rome. Tons of cards (165 cards, all uniquely named, but there are several duplicate powers) will cause great variability. RATING: 4.5
Another Feld that's just Ho-Hum. Actually, that's how I feel about every Feld game that was released after Trajan, at least the ones I played. There's lots of options, there are tons of ways to score points, but it's just not possible to go for a strategy as the randomness of the dealt cards is massive. This is a game where you want to build an engine early on so you can midway through or at the end start with your victory point multipliers. But you can't plan anything. In  agricola at least you have to make the best of the cards dealt IN THE BEGINNINg. The cards there stay with you for the rest of the game, but you play 4 out of 5 cards here, sometimes 5 out of 5 and get five new random cards at the start of the next round. There's not much room for saving up cards for later. Too bad if the cards you're dealt in the beginning are the ones to introduce victory point multipliers and the cards you get dealt at the end are the ones that build up an engine. The game definitely had its share of akward moments. Also, I strongly dislike the random market system. What if you don't happen to have cards with a high "retail value", as the "retail value" of the cards is determined randomly after you have drawn cards? That being said, it seems odd that I liked the implementation of yet another rather random mechanic, that is the inclusion of the threat tokens. Which is nothing else but the introduction and integration of a mild push-your-luck-element. Which (finding unique and innovative combinations of boardgame mechanics seems to be Felds most outstanding strength)) I find to be very clever and that results in a boardgame mix, that doesn't hurt too bad playing it and - despite feeling like a multiplayer solitaire's got a good flow. However, the longer I think about the session the more negative comes back to mind.

RATING: 9
Probably my favourite Feld and I am a Feld fan. This game is tactics rather than strategy. It is about using your hand of 5 cards to best effect each round and you have to be aware of what the other players are doing. There are three elements that give you bonus points if you have the majority even if it is just once in the game and even if you later lose it. Making a big play to get a majority early on and then letting it go can work well. Also staying ahead on something throughout the game so noone else can get the majority can be fun too. 

I love how threats balance against wealth ("I want to use this blue card to get 6 money (because the blue dice is on 6) but maybe I should use it to get rid of the blue threat I just took because the blue dice is on 6.")

16 plays in and not bored yet.

RATING: 10
A few years later, and I'm bumping this modern classic from a 9 to a 10. Once you're familiar with it, it plays in an hour or less (depending on the number of players), and I think the card mechanics and moderate level of player interaction provide something unique from the the rest of my Euro-style games. I understand and am empathic toward players who dislike the luck involved with the cards and dice rolls, but I think that's what seals it for me: Bruges is much more tactical than strategic, but paying attention to other players' actions, responding as best you can, and saving the right cards for later can help you gain an edge. I'm never disappointed with a round of this game, and almost always want to play if suggested. I feel that this is every bit as good as everyone's other favorite Feld, The Castles of Burgundy, but so completely different from it as to be a category unto itself.

RATING: 7
[thing=136888][/thing] is a light-ish game in which the cards are the heart and soul of the game - with them you do all the actions: build canals, to build buildings, use them to get rid of threats, or as persons (wich must be put in a building), to get money and to get workers. Every the players will use (at least) four cards, in one of the ways they work.

Once there are no more cards to be taken, it is the last round - the game is really short, around 1 hour, but with many things to consider in every turn. A clever game, as I expect from Feld - and, also, easy to learn, as none of us have problemas with rules, nor do we need to consult the manual during play (the way the cards work are described on them, and once we get used with the symbols and gameplay, is was a breeze).

There are dice, but they are used sort like they are in [thing=55670][/thing], as in: one roll for all of the dice, and they all will serve for all the players - the dice determin the amount of money the players will get (if the blue dice shows a 5, and you use a blue card to get money, you will get 5 money), how much cost to get influence (the sum of all the dice showing 1 and 2) and which threat markers the players will receive (when the dice shows a 5 or 6).

Though it isn't really a game with all that much interaction (the biggest part is in the majorities, where one can set the pace and blocks everyone else from gaining some nice points), in the plays I had we do had some actions that affected the others. For instance: in my first play, everyone went on different directions, using different combinations of persons. I went for canals, and suffered some setbacks due to persons of the others (one player use the Thief to steal money, other used the Arsenist to keep giving to the others red threat - fire - markers, and other used Vandals to destroy buildings of others), but still manage, in the end, to have some sort of engine working. In the end, a really close score: the winner had 45 points, I came in second, with 44, and the third place had 43 points! It was a great game.

The artwork, by Michael Menzel, is great.

So, for me, [thing=136888][/thing] is a solid light-ish game, with some deal of interaction (though not all that much, nor it will have issues with king-making, because the ill effects hits everyone, though, of course, someone may be more affected then others), in a play time of around 1 hour. I still prefer [thing=25554][/thing], for around the same length and weight, but [thing=136888][/thing] sure is a fine option.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
My initial concern about too many options at any moment (from watching rahdo's runthrough) is somewhat mitigated by the fact that you mostly won't be using cards' specially described abilities. To use such a card you first have to burn a different card to provide a house for it. And then you also burn cards to get money, workers, and canals, and to pay off threats.

It's aggravated though by the way that you play a single card on your turn. (Then after everyone has played four cards, that's the end of that round.) That makes it hard to solo play as you keep switching which player you are and it can be hard to keep a given player's strategy in mind. It's almost but not quite unnecessary that the game be structured this way, as most of what you do doesn't affect other players.

The point salad feel is stronger than I'd like. I think the problem is not just opacity (don't know what to try to do) but reduced tension (there always seems to be [i]something[/i] viable to do).

I don't mind the dice rolling since those results apply equally to everyone (see also: Macao). But as usual I don't care for the card drawing, into a personal hand, as this feels capricious and I worry about a feeling of low control.

The take-that abilities feel crude. Also, the seeming invitation to try to find game-winning card combos is unattractive.

Overall it's like a more random and much less thematic Pax Porfiriana.

...There's no focus to the competition with other players, such as the area control game or race game (shipping) in Macao. The disasters often lack bite (target something you don't have) and whether they hit you can come down to whether you draw the right color card. I don't know what's interesting here.

RATING: 7
Had a good time playing this game two player. With more, it should more or less offer the same experience, as the number of cards available is adjusted to the number of players. The Michael Menzel board is gorgeous, even though I am a bit tired of his style of portraits (beautiful but over used as far as I am concerned). If you, like me, have a fondness for the lighter Feld games (Speicherstadt, Macao, Castles Of Burgundy, Amerigo), then Brugge is right up your alley. It is much lighter fare, and much more straightforward, than Trajan or Bora Bora. The game is almost purely tactical (as opposed to strategic), so it is really about making the most of what your are dealt. Its main selling point for me is the interesting way in which all the unique cards (and there are many of them!) work together. I am also confident that the number of cards in the game will ensure replayability. All in all, a very pleasant game, but probably not a must-have if you own Macao or Castles Of Burgundy: it is a little bit less exciting.

RATING: 7
Another Feldian design with lots of special one time abilities that I quite enjoy. Special cards with unique abilities sometimes can be annoying when gaming with casual gamers who are not used to interpreting iconography. This makes the learning curve that much tougher and limits the gaming audience. However, underneath this typical Feldian design lies a fun game that feels more like a solo effort than a race for points. Money while tight, is not suffocating. There are ample ways of gaining resources either through card selection coupled with dice placement or playing varioud card combinations. Ultimately though, resources do need to be converted to points and there is where the competition exists in the form of building canals. Not sure why I like this one more. Perhaps unlike the other Feld designs where one has to fight tooth and nail for getting resources, this one just allows you to pick up the cards each hand for "free" and discard them for basic rewards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
After all the hype, I was hoping to love this game but, after a couple of plays, I find that we're just friends. 
Too random, too much AP (as you analyze your card options after drawing), and too much dependence on learning the uses of all 160-odd cards: keeping track of your opponent's tableaux is as important as knowing your own. Teeny-tiny-eye-straino-vision writing means if you want to know how your opponent's character cards can affect the game, you'll have to get up, walk around the table, and peer over their shoulder. Is too many cards a bad thing?
It's also remarkably difficult to plan long-term strategy. Short term knee-jerk tactics? Sure. But when you're only (usually) keeping 1 card between hands, every turn is spent reacting to your re-draw rather than putting a points engine in place. I wonder how different the game would feel with a play one / draw one structure? The first player marker would be static, with a dice re-roll every 4 rounds. "Extra play" abilities would be activated in a special round just prior to the dice roll...
All this aside, Bruges is a fun, light game with fantastic art that will never play the same twice. But I'm also glad it's a fairly short one.

RATING: 4
From a hand of 5, play 4 cards each turn.  Refresh.

Certain actions can be performed by every card, such as construct a house, collect workers (expended to construct houses), collect money, construct a canal segment on the board (requires money), etc.  But the focus of the cards is the personality portrait that provides a special power when placed into a constructed house.

[b]a)[/b] I am unwilling to invest time learning that much range of powers available

Slightly above average Euro with some uniquely interesting mechanics.   I really love the creeping doom of the threat tokens and the way Bruges uses dice drafting.  I just wish the activities were more interesting.  Canal building?  It's just point-grabbing.  Building tons of the same house?  More points... The color doesn't even matter.  The politics track?  Yet another points race.  It's stuff like this that holds Bruges back.  While I'm usually willing to play Bruges, I'd much rather play Feld's Bora Bora, Castles of Burgundy, or Trajan.

RATING: N/A
In this wealthy city in Belgium we are merchants trying to maintain our power and influence.  A beautiful game board depicting a city surrounding what appears to be a football field for prestige scoring. Along the outside perimeter of the city we need to dig a canal. Prosperity is threatened by fire, revolt, flood, intrigue, and plague. A 5-colored theme pervades the threat dice, multi-use cards, workers and areas of the city. Scoring for majority each round gives urgency to the game. Random card selection forces a diversified strategy.

RATING: 6
One of Feld's lighter contributions where you basically need to think on your feet. There is little to work with in long-term planning. It's all about collecting the most points, and trying to make the most of your draws to achieve that end.

It is not deep, but it does engage for the moment...indeed, it definitely plays out hand-to hand. There are a few Feldian ideas here, dice-determiners, and having to make the best of an immediate situation, but there is a subtle form of planning involved. 

This is basically a card game, each card having several options for use. You can always see the 'next' card color, but not it's abilities. You can prepare for situations that will give you better options with the cards you draw. So in that respect, it is not entirely 'luck of the draw'. Luck is just more apparent, but it does not navigate the game, it only looks like it does.

This is an easy game to teach, and it does play well with two, which is primarily how I play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I guess we’re true Feld-adepts. So far this year there are three new games published which are develloped by Stefan Feld. In the meantime we own, and played, all three of them. Bruges was the last one in the row. We expected that this would be a less interesting game than Rialto should be. As Rialto was a little bit disappointing, the expectations on Bruges were pretty low. Then we read a review which was quite possitive. The reviewer said it was even better then Bora Bora was, and if you wanted to buy one of the three new Feld games, this should be the one. We started to get very curious on the game, and M. started to read the German rules. I found an English translation of the rules, and I started to read those. After reading them, we believed this was a pretty easy game. And in fact, it isn’t that hard. It could be explained in 10 to 15 minutes, as there are just a couple of things to explain. Each round consists of 5 phases.


    Fill your hands up to 5 cards. There are two stacks of cards. Backside up. The cards have different colors, front and back. You have to choose which color will be the most usefull, and pick that card. You may not look at the front of the card until you have 5 cards in your hand.
    Roll the 5 dice. After rolling the dice, sort them on value from low to high, and place them on the square on the gameboard. Then distribute the threat markers for each die that shows a 5 or a 6, in the color of the die. At the moment a player receives the 3rd threat marker in a single color, the corresponding threat occurs immediately for that player. After the threat markers are distributed and the threats have occurred, the players may choose to advance 1 step on the prestige track. The costs to advance are determined by adding up all the pips of the dice that shows a 1 or a 2. This is the only time during a turn, a player can advance on the prestige track. If none of the dice show a 1 or a 2, then the players can’t advance in that round.
    Play cards and take actions.
    – Players start this phase with 5 cards in hand.
    – The players take turns playing 1 card from their hand, and select from 1 of the 6 possible actions.
    – This goes on in turns, until each player has played 4 cards from his hand, and executed the 4 corresponding actions.
    The possible actions are:
    a. Take 2 workers (corresponding to the color of the card)
    b. Take 1-6 florins (corresponding to the value of the die that correspond to the color of the card)
    c. Put back 1 threat marker (in the color of the card)
    d. Build 1 canal tile (if possible. The tile that is going to be build must match the color of the card.
    e. Build 1 house (pay with a worker corresponding to the color of the card)
    f. Place 1 person
    Determine the 3 majorities. When a player has a majority, he flips over the corresponding tile over to the colored side, and earns 4 points for that tile at the end of the game.

The major part of the game, is in playing the cards. And this is were the games become interesting. Each card shows a person which has unique possibilities, but you can’t use all of them because you’ll also need the other 5 possible actions. Then you’ll have to sacrifice that person, just because you need that red card to build the red canal tile or a blue card to collect some hard needed money, because the blue die is showing 6.  And the bad thing about all this, you know when you sacrifice a person, you won’t get them back in your hands, because all of the 165 cards in the game have unique persons on them.

After playing all we could say was: “WOW! This is a great game.” The, earlier mentioned, reviewer, could even be right, and, of the three Feld games that are published this year, this is the one to get. I say could be right, because there is quite a lot of luck in this game. There is luck in drawing cards, there is luck in rolling dice. But the luck is manageable, and that is what it makes a great game. But to say that it’s better then Bora Bora? That depends on the group which you’re playing. If you’re playing with a group that is slightly advanced in playing boardgames, this is the game to get. If you’re playing with experienced players, get Bora Bora, and if you’re playing with families or non-experienced players, just pick up Rialto. We’re lucky that we could have pick up all three of them, and don’t have to choose, but if we had to, it would be a hard choice between Bora Bora and Bruges. The players in us would pick the first one, but we know that we’ll play Bruges more often, just because it’s a bit lighter.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This game always leaves me wanting more. That, to me, is the hallmark of an excellent game. I love the card interaction and the excitement that comes with trying to draw a card to complete a combo. It plays quickly, smoothly, and I love the art. Winner!

EDIT: After repeated plays, I'm reducing the rating. I've found that all the criticisms are valid: too much randomness, too dependent on the luck-of-the-draw, card game masquerading as a board game. When the draw goes your way, the game is fantastic and a 9 as I wrote above. But when it doesn't it makes for a frustrating game lacking any interesting decision-making.

RATING: 8.5
Reminds me of Macao, another very good Feld title.  They both have a large library of "people" cards with activated/CIP/end-game-scoring abilities and poor, occasionally-confusing templating.  They both use dice in a smart, non-annoying way.  

But between Macao's cool wind rose mechanic and the fact that Bruges plays in half the time that Macao does, they don't feel redundant.
